<span id="freeTextContainer11174942323104858335">A difficult one.<br/><br/>Worldbuilding: excellent, honestly. I could walk you through Ambergris right now.<br/>Language and style: ah, what a wonder.<br/><br/>I'm not really sure what I enjoyed more. The writing or the world.<br/><br/>Storytelling: Well... You know, a bright new world is a good thing and painting that world with all those magnificent words an even better one. But there has to be a story somewhere in a book of several hundred pages.<br/>There has to be at least one likeable person, flawed like everyone of us mortals, </span>
  <span id="freeText11174942323104858335" style="display:none">A difficult one.<br /><br />Worldbuilding: excellent, honestly. I could walk you through Ambergris right now.<br />Language and style: ah, what a wonder.<br /><br />I'm not really sure what I enjoyed more. The writing or the world.<br /><br />Storytelling: Well... You know, a bright new world is a good thing and painting that world with all those magnificent words an even better one. But there has to be a story somewhere in a book of several hundred pages.<br />There has to be at least one likeable person, flawed like everyone of us mortals, who makes really stupid decisions that lead to something interesting which then may or may not be the plot of the aforementioned story.<br /><br />After aprox half of the book that story was nowhere to be found. I hope to find it in one of the later Ambergris works.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1314885730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2463370066328761594">This 700 page book took me a day and a half to read. The penultimate story in this novel was the last thing I read last night and the first thing I thought about when I woke up this morning. Driving home from errands, I found myself thinking about it again, along with thoughts of David Foster Wallace, and tearing up. This review might take awhile. In the meantime, this book is awesome, and you should probably read it.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2439950347" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2439950347" />
    <a title="Leo Robertson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7619789-leo-robertson"><img alt="Leo Robertson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1571602096p2/7619789.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2439950347?book_show_action=true">Jun 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leo Robertson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leo Robertson" href="/user/show/7619789-leo-robertson">Leo Robertson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781509848157" href="/book/show/35658721-city-of-saints-and-madmen">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2439950347" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8709012908838605871">If you want metafictional/autobiographical dark fantasy, read "Lanark" by Alasdair Gray instead. (I know you could probably read both, but don't.)<br/><br/>After I finished the first novella in this collection, I was so excited by the prospect of a full book of novellas like it. Even though I thought this was one big novel in its own right—it's actually a loosely linked collection of novellas in the city of Ambergris. Okay fine, so I couldn't lose myself in the world like I thought I'd be able to, but a d</span>
  <span id="freeText8709012908838605871" style="display:none">If you want metafictional/autobiographical dark fantasy, read "Lanark" by Alasdair Gray instead. (I know you could probably read both, but don't.)<br /><br />After I finished the first novella in this collection, I was so excited by the prospect of a full book of novellas like it. Even though I thought this was one big novel in its own right—it's actually a loosely linked collection of novellas in the city of Ambergris. Okay fine, so I couldn't lose myself in the world like I thought I'd be able to, but a different fun to be had.<br /><br />Not really though!<br /><br />So, the first novella is called "Dradin, In Love", and it's beautiful, surreal and horrifying. The otherworldly nature of the story perfectly fits the fascinating and amazingly evoked city of Ambergris. A weird whaling world. It just seems like peak Vandermeer for sure—before the category of weird starts to devour itself.<br />"Hello I'm strange and my writing is bizarre."<br />Like, gimme a break. Weird people who do weird work, authentically, are not weird unto themselves, so the idea of someone declaring themselves "weird" automatically disqualifies them, to me.<br /><br />The second novella I quit. I already knew Vandermeer had gone through a Nabokov phase and this one "does a Pale Fire." (Excessive footnotes and all that. Who can be buggered, honestly?) I enjoyed Pale Fire the first time around, because when I was younger I had a higher literary pain threshold. The second time I attempted it I was like UGH. So the first time I attempted this novella I started at TRIPLE UGH. (And I think it was written in the style of a 19th century author as well—dude...)<br /><br />The third novella, The Transformation of Martin Lake, is about an artist who receives "an invitation to a beheading"—the name of a Nabokov book.<br />It's not bad. I made it to the end but it had to top the first novella, which set a high bar.<br /><br />After novella 4 I became more excited by the prospect of not having to read this anymore. The Strange Case of X, it's called (I mean, no thanks, right? Saramago meets SaramaNO.) It's about the author of "City of Saints and Madmen" about how he's a writer in Chicago and he dreamed up the city of Ambergris. So once again, "No Leo, I will not let you lose yourself in my city. Rather, I will indulge in a meandering, pointless, overdone metafictional game that is of interest to who, exactly? Who could possibly give AF?" It talks about the sales of the book, about what happened to the author afterwards... and so I think, not only don't I care but I can't actually make sense out of what this novella is trying to tell me. Did this happen or not? Was this added after this book was published? Well, I don't care enough to look that up, and I don't get what the point would be if it was or wasn't.<br /><br />Then I realised I could get cracking on not having to read it anymore straight away by just quitting. I'm so mad about having to do that, because I don't think I've finished a 500+pg book in years. Is my stamina decreasing or are there very few books that justify going to that page length and beyond?<br /><br />I just don't exactly know what Vandermeer's point is with the Nabokov/other-author aping, and I feel a little dismayed by it. If you're the king of the highly inventive genre, what's inventive about proving you can do what someone else invented? Is it to take conventions from other writers and pass them off as your own for audiences who may not be familiar with their sources?<br /><br />Besides, old man wordplay and text games (Borges Nabokov etc) make me think these guys were fortunate enough to find a new form of fun after going impotent or something. "After I lost penile strength I was on a downer for months. Luckily, alliteration lol." (I know that's an entirely useless association. It's like when someone told me they hated the word "proboscis" because it sounded like the technical term for a dog's erection. But once the association is there, it's not leaving your head.)<br /><br />Also the page numbers kept changing and not even just because they're different stories/relics of Ambergris. But because someone doesn't know how to edit a fucking Word document. That's real weird!<br /><br />Check out that cover though!!</span>
  <a data-text-id="8709012908838605871" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_503192675" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/503192675" />
    <a title="Pixelina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5366052-pixelina"><img alt="Pixelina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1464094222p2/5366052.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/503192675?book_show_action=true">Jan 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Pixelina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Pixelina" href="/user/show/5366052-pixelina">Pixelina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5366052-pixelina?shelf=z2014-read">z2014-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5366052-pixelina?shelf=short-stories">short-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5366052-pixelina?shelf=fantasy">fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5366052-pixelina?shelf=mount-tbr">mount-tbr</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5366052-pixelina?shelf=weird">weird</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer503192675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13624275560153838442">This book is an adventure in the house of mirrors where stories and people touch and slightly distort and echo back.<br/><br/>I struggled a bit with parts of it (mainly the rather boring religious elements) but the rest of it was just so interesting. I especially loved the last story about the copywriter in search of the perfect sentence while being haunted by dwarfs.<br/><br/>Oh I hear there is another Ambergris book too!</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3105065319" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3105065319" />
    <a title="Oliver" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106361163-oliver"><img alt="Oliver" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1575681021p2/106361163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3105065319?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Oliver" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Oliver" href="/user/show/106361163-oliver">Oliver</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/22590838-city-of-saints-and-madmen">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3105065319" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2938989747709726525">I really, really, REALLY wish this book was good. Unfortunately, it sucks. <br/><br/>The structure of this book is fascinating, and I haven’t really seen something like it: I saw a Goodreads review of the next book in the series describe this one as “patchwork,” if I recall correctly, and that’s an absolutely apt word to use. It’s a portrait of a fictional city—Ambergris— that has been Frankensteined together using alternate history, alternative biology, short story, letters, and magazine articles. At one</span>
  <span id="freeText2938989747709726525" style="display:none">I really, really, REALLY wish this book was good. Unfortunately, it sucks. <br /><br />The structure of this book is fascinating, and I haven’t really seen something like it: I saw a Goodreads review of the next book in the series describe this one as “patchwork,” if I recall correctly, and that’s an absolutely apt word to use. It’s a portrait of a fictional city—Ambergris— that has been Frankensteined together using alternate history, alternative biology, short story, letters, and magazine articles. At one point, a story was even told through a fictional bibliography. This was really cool (possibly apart from the bibliography, which drove me nuts), and I think it was one of the book’s few saving graces. It meant that whenever I got bored, I knew there would be a structure change soon. The novelty and variety kept it... if not <i>interesting</i>, exactly, then at least manageable. However, I think that maybe knowing it’s going to end soon being the primary motivator to keep me reading just might not be indicative of the best writing. <br /><br />This is not to say City was without merit. There were parts that I sincerely enjoyed. There were even parts I thought were genius. I gave this book an entire star above the absolute minimum, after all— something must have outweighed the absolute horribleness that was the rest of the book. That something was the short story “The Transformation of Martin Lake.”<br /><br />Transformation was the only point where I felt that the patchwork nature of the storytelling actually had thematic weight besides just novelty value. It tells the story of a young artist, Martin Lake, and the transformation of his art from kind of mediocre to genius. It does this via the alternation of a typical short story narration, focused on Lake’s point of view, and a series of excerpts from an art critics’ publication. You get the story from two different sides: you get what actually happened to Lake to make him transform, and subsequently you understand what all the rest of his art means. Meanwhile, Janice Shriek’s description of Lake’s art is evocative and inspiring, and provides another perspective on the emotional meaning of his work, even while she has no idea about the meat of the story: the true nature of Lake’s transformation. I am not spoiling this story because (excepting a few references to Ambergris history) I think it can be read as a stand-alone, and I would encourage anyone who is interested to read Transformation if they ever find themself with access to a copy of City. The story was (at risk of sounding dramatic) transcendent and beautiful. <br /><br />This is why it so shocked me and disappointed me when the book immediately followed up with one of the worst short stories I’ve ever read. At this point, I am going to use spoilers. If anyone who has not read the book but has read Transformation wishes to remain in that happy haze of pretending or assuming the rest of the book is just as good, I would encourage them to 1) stop reading this review 2) not read any more of the book. <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I figured, as soon as I read the title, that “The Strange Case of X” was the short story mentioned in the summary of the book about a person who was in a Chicago insane asylum for believing Ambergris was real. I assumed, at first, when I read that summary, that I would hate it and be deeply annoyed with it; however, after reading Transformation, I placed a lot more trust in the book and decided that it was worth a try. <br /><br />It was not. <br /><br />If the description sounds terrible, I promise the actual story was worse. My first real clue was when the perspective switched, for no discernible reason, from third to first person. My second real clue was when the “insane person” who “believed Ambergris was real” was Jeff VanderMeer, referred to as “X”. Like, his actual self insert. Not as, “this is kinda, I’m assuming, an author self-insert and I can work it out through context clues.” No, he quotes himself. He has explicitly written himself into the story as a character, and furthermore, <i>as the author of the story</i>. I understand why people hate metafiction now. <br /><br />I guessed the twist immediately and was right: the twist being that it was still set in Ambergris and the real world was actually the one that X had made up, except he doesn’t realize because he’s craaaaaazy! If X was some sort of self-aware parody of VanderMeer, then it didn’t come across as such. I did not sign up to read this book for anecdotes about VanderMeer’s childhood presented in doctor interview format. It felt self-centered and arrogant in a way that’s really hard to describe, and was generally kind of insufferable. To make matters worse, this section made it really clear that in-universe, this was how the rest of the book was written: it was the ravings of a lunatic who was locked in a cell in a city he thought he made up. This presentation of mental health (specifically hallucinations and delusions but also depression and childhood trauma) was infuriating. It felt like it had been written by someone who thought the abridged pop-culture “we’re all mad here!” version of Alice in Wonderland was the best story ever told. There were jokes about how therapy sucks and is a scam but also jokes about how nobody was mature enough to just go to therapy. It felt deeply under-edited.<br /><br />From that point forwards, the book felt like a cruel joke. The two sections that came before Transformation— “Dradin, in Love,” something I am choosing to interpret as an absurdist comment on the entitlement of missionaries, and “The Hoegbutton Guide to the Early History of Ambergris,” an extremely tedious (but admittedly funny) alternate history depicting the rise of the city of Ambergris (it had 137 footnotes)— were kind of manageable, and I did end up being invested in them, despite the fact that they were, ultimately, kind of boring. After X, once I’d lost all interest and investment (how to suspend disbelief, when the story explicitly states within its own world that it is fictional?), this long and tedious style was near impossible to care about. And just in case you forgot that this whole thing was being written by VanderMeer, from within the cell of an insane asylum within Ambergris, references and reminders of that fact are peppered throughout. Every character is a man with a wife or girlfriend in the background (this book does not pass the Bechdel test, and every single narrator except Janice Shriek is a man, of course) who is struggling to write something. They all become self-inserts. It just isn’t interesting— I’d go so far as to call it exhausting.<br /><br />Perhaps my assessment is unfair. Maybe one short story shouldn’t be enough to ruin the entire book. Maybe I missed something thematically and there is actually a genius revelation about the human condition right there on the page that I just didn’t see. I really cannot bring myself to go back and check. It may be true that I just didn’t Get It, but VanderMeer didn’t give me any reason why I should pay attention enough to try. Deeply disappointing.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="2938989747709726525" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_9490481" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/9490481" />
    <a title="Nathanimal" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/436949-nathanimal"><img alt="Nathanimal" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547243047p2/436949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/9490481?book_show_action=true">Nov 24, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathanimal" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathanimal" href="/user/show/436949-nathanimal">Nathanimal</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer9490481" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14827026772595612898">I really wanted to like this book, and fantastic things did happen, as promised. I really liked the ritual murder with the bird masks (though the main character's untrue emotions and reactions during that part kept un-riveting me). I liked the story about the writer who lived in two worlds (though the gotchya ending was kind of an eye-roller). And I liked the mushroom people and the king squid material. I think this novel was supposed to be a marriage between fantasy and meta-po-mo writing like </span>
  <span id="freeText14827026772595612898" style="display:none">I really wanted to like this book, and fantastic things did happen, as promised. I really liked the ritual murder with the bird masks (though the main character's untrue emotions and reactions during that part kept un-riveting me). I liked the story about the writer who lived in two worlds (though the gotchya ending was kind of an eye-roller). And I liked the mushroom people and the king squid material. I think this novel was supposed to be a marriage between fantasy and meta-po-mo writing like that of Borges and Calvino. That concept really excites me. However, the prosy, pulpy language that most of the novel is written in constantly distracted me from the fictive dream. And the universe of nesting chinese boxes offered by those other meta-po-mo writers is much more profound. I wondered if I should cut Vandermeer some slack. After all the genre "fantasy" was printed right on the spine. Maybe I expected too much. I tried telling myself, It's just for fun. But ultimately I couldn't have the fun I wanted to have. Also, while the post-modern multi-faceted narrative approach was interesting, it failed to coalesce for me into something great, like, say, Colson Whitehead's *John Henry Days*. With each new narrative I was challenged to find something to be interested in. And that, I think, is the problem with this kind of writing. There's never a very strong story, when you're jumping around from narrative to narrative without returning. It's a neat way to describe a place, but ultimately description is not a story. And if the individual pieces don't stand on their own then, well, the whole novel only gets two stars. I'm sorry, Jeff Vandermeer. I'm not saying I could do better, and I know you put a lot of effort and imagination into this, but two starts it is.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14827026772595612898" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('edLYhTgf6lg7eiYTk4aYUFUqDIjUl0B7w3ByBmPKc0P+l9s2ofxXkrSO5mBGoIl0wXCr0l4hHMd2inZ0r5GSFQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cisEbYxwRtMuy+6aBlB4SG5sC8IHoiAD0csj0YArfnz1bgfeFZP7GaE/LunTdmls+jasmI0UfL9kMSejTHCfKg==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/33919.Jeff_VanderMeer">About Jeff VanderMeer</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/33919.Jeff_VanderMeer"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1522640540p3/33919.jpg);'></div>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7250389606464634811">NYT bestselling writer Jeff VanderMeer has been called “the weird Thoreau” by the New Yorker for his engagement with ecological issues. His most recent novel, the national bestseller Borne, received wide-spread critical acclaim and his prior novels include the Southern Reach trilogy (Annihilation, Authority, and Acceptance). Annihilation won the Nebula and Shirley Jackson Awards, has been translat</span>
  <span id="freeText7250389606464634811" style="display:none">NYT bestselling writer Jeff VanderMeer has been called “the weird Thoreau” by the New Yorker for his engagement with ecological issues. His most recent novel, the national bestseller Borne, received wide-spread critical acclaim and his prior novels include the Southern Reach trilogy (Annihilation, Authority, and Acceptance). Annihilation won the Nebula and Shirley Jackson Awards, has been translated into 35 languages, and was made into a film from Paramount Pictures directed by Alex Garland. His nonfiction has appeared in New York Times, the Los Angeles Times, the Atlantic, Slate, Salon, and the Washington Post. He has coedited several iconic anthologies with his wife, the Hugo Award winning editor. Other titles include Wonderbook, the world’s first fully illustrated creative writing guide. VanderMeer served as the 2016-2017 Trias Writer in Residence at Hobart and William Smith Colleges. He has spoken at the Guggenheim, the Library of Congress, and the Arthur C. Clarke Center for the Human Imagination. <br /><br />VanderMeer was born in Bellefonte, Pennsylvania, but spent much of his childhood in the Fiji Islands, where his parents worked for the Peace Corps. This experience, and the resulting trip back to the United States through Asia, Africa, and Europe, deeply influenced him.<br /><br />Jeff is married to Ann VanderMeer, who is currently an acquiring editor at Tor.com and has won the Hugo Award and World Fantasy Award for her editing of magazines and anthologies. They live in Tallahassee, Florida, with two cats and thousands of books.<br /><br /></span>

Narrated with flamboyant intensity and under increasingly urgent conditions by ex-society figure Janice Shriek, this afterword presents a vivid gallery of characters and events, emphasizing the adventures of Janice\'s brother Duncan, a historian obsessed with a doomed love affair and a secret that may kill or transform him; a war between rival publishing houses that will change Ambergris forever; and the gray caps, a marginalized people armed with advanced fungal technologies who have been waiting underground for their chance to mold the future of the city.
